<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Indus Valley Civilisation</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Bronze Age civilisation in South Asia</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><caption class="infobox-title">Indus Valley Civilisation</caption><tbody><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_Civilization,_Mature_Phase_(2600-1900_BCE).png"><img alt="IVC major sites" <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mohenjo-daro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mohenjo-daro.jpg/220px-Mohenjo-daro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mohenjo-daro.jpg/330px-Mohenjo-daro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mohenjo-daro.jpg/440px-Mohenjo-daro.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mohenjo-daro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Excavated ruins of <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> province, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, showing the <a href="/wiki/Great_Bath,_Mohenjo-daro" title="Great Bath, Mohenjo-daro">Great Bath</a> in the foreground. Mohenjo-daro, on the right bank of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>, the first site in South Asia to be so declared.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harappan_small_figures.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="667" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Harappan_small_figures.jpg/220px-Harappan_small_figures.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Harappan_small_figures.jpg/330px-Harappan_small_figures.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Harappan_small_figures.jpg/440px-Harappan_small_figures.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harappan_small_figures.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature votive images or toy models from <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a>, c. 2500 BCE. <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">Terracotta</a> figurines indicate the yoking of <a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">zebu</a> oxen for pulling a cart and the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a>, a domesticated jungle fowl.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Indus Valley Civilisation</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>  (<b>IVC</b>), also known as the <b>Indus Civilisation</b> was a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civilisation" title="Civilisation">civilisation</a> in the northwestern regions of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, lasting from 3300 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a> to 1300 BCE, and in its mature form from 2600 BCE to 1900 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20091_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20091-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> Together with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, it was one of three early civilisations of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, and of the three, the most widespread.  Its sites spanned an area from northeast <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and much of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> to western and northwestern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright2009_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright2009-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[b]</a></sup> The civilisation flourished both in the <a href="/wiki/Alluvial_plain" title="Alluvial plain">alluvial plain</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>, which flows through the length of Pakistan, and along a system of perennial <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>-fed rivers that once coursed in the vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Ghaggar-Hakra_River" title="Ghaggar-Hakra River">Ghaggar-Hakra</a>, a seasonal river in northwest India and eastern Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20091_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20091-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiosanCliftMacklinFuller2012_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiosanCliftMacklinFuller2012-6">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cities of the ancient Indus were noted for their <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baked_brick" title="Baked brick">baked brick</a> houses, elaborate <a href="/wiki/Drainage" title="Drainage">drainage</a> systems, <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> systems, clusters of large non-residential buildings, and techniques of handicraft and <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[c]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a> very likely grew to contain between 30,000 and 60,000 individuals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[6]</a></sup> and the civilisation may have contained between one and five million individuals during its florescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcIntosh2008387_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcIntosh2008387-10">[7]</a></sup>  A gradual <a href="/wiki/Aridification" title="Aridification">drying</a> of the region during the 3rd millennium BCE may have been the initial stimulus for its urbanisation. Eventually it also reduced the water supply enough to cause the civilisation's demise and to disperse its population to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Note-Brooke_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Note-Brooke-11">[d]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indus civilisation is also known as the <b>Harappan civilisation</b>, after its <a href="/wiki/Type_site" title="Type site">type site</a> Harappa, the first to be excavated early in the 20th century in what was then the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_Province_(British_India)" title="Punjab Province (British India)">Punjab province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">British India</a> and is now <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab, Pakistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHabib201513_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHabib201513-12">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[e]</a></sup> The discovery of Harappa and soon afterwards Mohenjo-daro was the culmination of work that had begun after the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Survey_of_India" title="Archaeological Survey of India">Archaeological Survey of India</a> in the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a> in 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20092_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20092-14">[9]</a></sup> There were earlier and later cultures called Early Harappan and Late Harappan in the same area. The early Harappan cultures were populated from <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> cultures, the earliest and best-known of which is <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a>, Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaffer_1992_loc=I:441–464,_II:425–446_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shaffer_1992_loc=I:441–464,_II:425–446-15">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenoyer1991_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenoyer1991-16">[11]</a></sup> Harappan civilisation is sometimes called <b>Mature Harappan</b> to distinguish it from the earlier cultures.
</p><p>Although over a thousand Mature Harappan sites have been reported and nearly a hundred excavated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPossehl2002a_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPossehl2002a-17">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[f]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPossehl200220_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPossehl200220-20">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008-p137_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-p137-21">[15]</a></sup> there are five major urban centres:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015192_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015192-22">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[g]</a></sup>  Mohenjo-daro in the lower Indus Valley (declared a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> in 1980 as "Archaeological Ruins at Moenjodaro"), Harappa in the western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab region</a>, Ganeriwala in the <a href="/wiki/Cholistan_Desert" title="Cholistan Desert">Cholistan Desert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dholavira" title="Dholavira">Dholavira</a> in western Gujarat (declared a UNESCO World Heritage Site in 2021 as "Dholavira: A Harappan City"), and <a href="/wiki/Rakhigarhi" title="Rakhigarhi">Rakhigarhi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright2009107_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright2009107-24">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[h]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harappan_language" title="Harappan language">Harappan language</a> is not directly attested, and its affiliation uncertain as the <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">Indus script</a> has remained undeciphered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[18]</a></sup> A relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Elamo-Dravidian_languages" title="Elamo-Dravidian languages">Elamo-Dravidian</a> language family is favoured by a section of scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERatnagar2006a25_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERatnagar2006a25-27">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Indus civilisation is named after the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a> <a href="/wiki/Drainage_system_(geomorphology)" title="Drainage system (geomorphology)">river system</a> in whose <a href="/wiki/Alluvial_plain" title="Alluvial plain">alluvial plains</a> the early sites of the civilisation were identified and excavated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright200910_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright200910-29">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[i]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following a tradition in archaeology, the civilisation is sometimes referred to as the <i>Harappan,</i> after its <a href="/wiki/Type_site" title="Type site">type site</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a>, the first site to be excavated in the 1920s; this is notably true of usage employed by the Archaeological Survey of India after India's independence in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHabib200213–14_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHabib200213–14-31">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[j]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Ghaggar-Hakra" figures prominently in modern labels applied to the Indus civilisation on account of a good number of sites having been found along the <a href="/wiki/Ghaggar-Hakra_River" title="Ghaggar-Hakra River">Ghaggar-Hakra River</a> in northwest India and eastern Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPossehl2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXVgeAAAAQBAJpgPA8_8–11]_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPossehl2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXVgeAAAAQBAJpgPA8_8–11]-33">[23]</a></sup> The terms "Indus-Sarasvati Civilisation" and "Sindhu-Saraswati Civilisation" have also been employed in the literature after a posited identification of the Ghaggar-Hakra with the river <a href="/wiki/Sarasvati_River" title="Sarasvati River">Saraswati</a> described in the early chapters of <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, a collection of hymns in <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">archaic Sanskrit</a> composed in the second-millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-34">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHabib200244_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHabib200244-35">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent geophysical research suggests that unlike the <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Sarasvati</a>, whose descriptions in the Rig Veda are those of a snow-fed river, the Ghaggar-Hakra was a system of perennial monsoon-fed rivers, which became seasonal around the time that the civilisation diminished, approximately 4,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiosanCliftMacklinFuller2012_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiosanCliftMacklinFuller2012-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[k]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IVC-major-sites-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1548" data-file-width="1259" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/IVC-major-sites-2.jpg/220px-IVC-major-sites-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/IVC-major-sites-2.jpg/330px-IVC-major-sites-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/IVC-major-sites-2.jpg/440px-IVC-major-sites-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IVC-major-sites-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major sites and extent of the Indus Valley Civilisation</div></div></div>
<p>The Indus Valley Civilisation was roughly contemporary with the other riverine civilisations of the ancient world: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> in the lands watered by the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in the drainage basin of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a>. By the time of its mature phase, the civilisation had spread over an area larger than the others, which included a core of 1,500 kilometres (900 mi) up the alluvial plain of the Indus and its tributaries. In addition, there was a region with disparate flora, fauna, and habitats, up to ten times as large, which had been shaped culturally and economically by the Indus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher201835_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher201835-37">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[l]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 6500 BCE, agriculture emerged in <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a>, on the margins of the Indus alluvium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson201829_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson201829-39">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[m]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher201833_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher201833-41">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[n]</a></sup> In the following millennia, settled life made inroads into the Indus plains, setting the stage for the growth of rural and urban human settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015138_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015138-43">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[o]</a></sup> The more organized sedentary life, in turn, led to a net increase in the birth rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson201829_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson201829-39">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[p]</a></sup> The large urban centres of Mohenjo-daro and Harappa very likely grew to containing between 30,000 and 60,000 individuals, and during the civilisation's florescence, the population of the subcontinent grew to between 4–6 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson201829_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson201829-39">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[q]</a></sup> During this period the death rate increased as well, for close living conditions of humans and domesticated animals led to an increase in contagious diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher201833_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher201833-41">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[r]</a></sup> According to one estimate, the population of the Indus civilisation at its peak may have been between one and five million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcIntosh2008186–187_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcIntosh2008186–187-48">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[s]</a></sup>
</p><p>The civilisation extended from Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Balochistan,_Pakistan" title="Balochistan, Pakistan">Balochistan</a> in the west to India's western <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> in the east, from northeastern Afghanistan in the north to India's <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> state in the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-34">[24]</a></sup> The largest number of sites are in <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(state)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (state)">Jammu and Kashmir</a> states in India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-34">[24]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">Balochistan</a> provinces in Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-34">[24]</a></sup> Coastal settlements extended from <a href="/wiki/Sutkagan_Dor" title="Sutkagan Dor">Sutkagan Dor</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[31]</a></sup> in Western Baluchistan to <a href="/wiki/Lothal" title="Lothal">Lothal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[32]</a></sup> in Gujarat. An Indus Valley site has been found on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus_River" title="Oxus River">Oxus River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Shortugai" title="Shortugai">Shortugai</a> in northern Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenoyer199896_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenoyer199896-52">[33]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Gomal_River" title="Gomal River">Gomal River</a> valley in northwestern Pakistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[34]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Manda,_Jammu" title="Manda, Jammu">Manda, Jammu</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Jammu" title="Jammu">Jammu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[35]</a></sup> India, and at <a href="/wiki/Alamgirpur" title="Alamgirpur">Alamgirpur</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Hindon_River" title="Hindon River">Hindon River</a>, only 28 km (17 mi) from Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[36]</a></sup> The southernmost site of the Indus Valley Civilisation is <a href="/wiki/Daimabad" title="Daimabad">Daimabad</a> in Maharashtra. Indus Valley sites have been found most often on rivers, but also on the ancient seacoast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[37]</a></sup> for example, Balakot (<a href="/wiki/Kot_Bala" title="Kot Bala">Kot Bala</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[38]</a></sup> and on islands, for example, <a href="/wiki/Dholavira" title="Dholavira">Dholavira</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_history_of_excavation">Discovery and history of excavation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Discovery and history of excavation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1859" data-file-width="1386" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg/170px-Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg/255px-Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg/340px-Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Cunningham_of_the_ASI_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Cunningham" title="Alexander Cunningham">Alexander Cunningham</a>, the first director general of the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Survey_of_India" title="Archaeological Survey of India">Archaeological Survey of India</a> (ASI), interpreted a Harappan <a href="/wiki/Stamp_seal" title="Stamp seal">stamp seal</a> in 1875.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="275" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg/170px-Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg/255px-Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/R._D._Banerji" title="R. D. Banerji">R. D. Banerji</a>, an officer of the ASI, visited Mohenjo-daro in 1919–1920, and again in 1922–1923, postulating the site's far-off antiquity.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="306" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg/170px-John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg/255px-John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Hubert_Marshall_-_Cyclopedia_of_India_1906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_(archaeologist)" title="John Marshall (archaeologist)">John Marshall</a>, the director-general of the ASI from 1902 to 1928, who oversaw the excavations in Harappa and Mohenjo-daro, shown in a 1906 photograph</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:16em; border-width: 1px; font-size: 85%; background-color: #F5DEB3;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>"Three other scholars whose names I cannot pass over in silence, are the late Mr. <a href="/wiki/R._D._Banerji" title="R. D. Banerji">R. D. Banerji</a>, to whom belongs the credit of having discovered, if not Mohenjo-daro itself, at any rate its high antiquity, and his immediate successors in the task of excavation, Messrs. <a href="/wiki/Madho_Sarup_Vats" title="Madho Sarup Vats">M.S. Vats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kashinath_Narayan_Dikshit" title="Kashinath Narayan Dikshit">K.N. Dikshit</a>. ... no one probably except myself can fully appreciate the difficulties and hardships which they had to face in the three first seasons at Mohenjo-daro"
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style=""> — From, John Marshall (ed), <i>Mohenjo-daro and the Indus Civilization</i>, London: Arthur Probsthain, 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1931x_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1931x-59">[40]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The first modern accounts of the <a href="/wiki/Ruins" title="Ruins">ruins</a> of the Indus civilisation are those of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Masson" title="Charles Masson">Charles Masson</a>, a deserter from the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>'s army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup> In 1829, Masson traveled through the <a href="/wiki/Princely_state" title="Princely state">princely state</a> of Punjab, gathering useful intelligence for the Company in return for a promise of clemency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup> An aspect of this arrangement was the additional requirement to hand over to the Company any historical artifacts acquired during his travels. Masson, who had versed himself in the <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a>, especially in the military campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, chose for his wanderings some of the same towns that had featured in Alexander's campaigns, and whose archaeological sites had been noted by the campaign's chroniclers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup> Masson's major archaeological discovery in the Punjab was Harappa, a metropolis of the Indus civilisation in the valley of Indus's tributary, the <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi river</a>. Masson made copious notes and illustrations of Harappa's rich historical artifacts, many lying half-buried. In 1842, Masson included his observations of Harappa in the book <i>Narrative of Various Journeys in Baluchistan, Afghanistan, and the Punjab</i>. He dated the Harappa ruins to a period of recorded history, erroneously mistaking it to have been described earlier during Alexander's campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup> Masson was impressed by the site's extraordinary size and by several large mounds formed from long-existing erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[t]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two years later, the Company contracted Alexander Burnes to sail up the Indus to assess the feasibility of water travel for its army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup> Burnes, who also stopped in Harappa, noted the baked bricks employed in the site's ancient masonry, but noted also the haphazard plundering of these bricks by the local population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6_60-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20095–6-60">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite these reports, Harappa was raided even more perilously for its bricks after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Sikh_War" title="Second Anglo-Sikh War">British annexation of the Punjab</a> in 1848–49. A considerable number were carted away as <a href="/wiki/Track_ballast" title="Track ballast">track ballast</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Track_(rail_transport)" title="Track (rail transport)">railway lines</a> being laid in the Punjab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20096_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20096-63">[43]</a></sup> Nearly 160 km (100 mi) of railway track between <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, laid in the mid 1850s, was supported by Harappan bricks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20096_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20096-63">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1861, three years after the dissolution of the East India Company and the establishment of <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">Crown rule in India</a>, archaeology on the subcontinent became more formally organised with the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Survey_of_India" title="Archaeological Survey of India">Archaeological Survey of India</a> (ASI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20096–7_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20096–7-64">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Cunningham" title="Alexander Cunningham">Alexander Cunningham</a>, the Survey's first director-general, who had visited Harappa in 1853 and had noted the imposing brick walls, visited again to carry out a survey, but this time of a site whose entire upper layer had been stripped in the interim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20096–7_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20096–7-64">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015180_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015180-65">[45]</a></sup> Although his original goal of demonstrating Harappa to be a lost Buddhist city mentioned in the seventh century CE travels of the Chinese visitor, <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a>, proved elusive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015180_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015180-65">[45]</a></sup> Cunningham did publish his findings in 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20097_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20097-66">[46]</a></sup> For the first time, he interpreted a Harappan <a href="/wiki/Stamp_seal" title="Stamp seal">stamp seal</a>, with its unknown script, which he concluded to be of an origin foreign to India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20097_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20097-66">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunningham1875pp._[httpsarchiveorgstreaminernetdli20155472202015547220Archaeological-Surbeypagen115mode2up_105]–108_and_pl._32–33_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunningham1875pp._[httpsarchiveorgstreaminernetdli20155472202015547220Archaeological-Surbeypagen115mode2up_105]–108_and_pl._32–33-67">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeological work in Harappa thereafter lagged until a new viceroy of India, <a href="/wiki/George_Curzon,_1st_Marquess_Curzon_of_Kedleston" title="George Curzon, 1st Marquess Curzon of Kedleston">Lord Curzon</a>, pushed through the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Monuments_Preservation_Act_1904" title="Ancient Monuments Preservation Act 1904">Ancient Monuments Preservation Act 1904</a>, and appointed <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_(archaeologist)" title="John Marshall (archaeologist)">John Marshall</a> to lead the ASI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20098_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20098-68">[48]</a></sup> Several years later, <a href="/wiki/Hiranand_Sastri" title="Hiranand Sastri">Hiranand Sastri</a>, who had been assigned by Marshall to survey Harappa, reported it to be of non-Buddhist origin, and by implication more ancient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20098_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20098-68">[48]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expropriation" title="Expropriation">Expropriating</a> Harappa for the ASI under the Act, Marshall directed ASI archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Daya_Ram_Sahni" title="Daya Ram Sahni">Daya Ram Sahni</a> to excavate the site's two mounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20098_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20098-68">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Farther south, along the <a href="/wiki/Main_stem" title="Main stem">main stem</a> of the Indus in <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sind</a> province, the largely undisturbed site of <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a> had attracted notice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20098_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20098-68">[48]</a></sup> Marshall deputed a succession of ASI officers to survey the site. These included <a href="/wiki/D._R._Bhandarkar" title="D. R. Bhandarkar">D. R. Bhandarkar</a> (1911), <a href="/wiki/R._D._Banerji" title="R. D. Banerji">R. D. Banerji</a> (1919, 1922–1923), and <a href="/wiki/Madho_Sarup_Vats" title="Madho Sarup Vats">M. S. Vats</a> (1924).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20098–9_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20098–9-69">[49]</a></sup> In 1923, on his second visit to Mohenjo-daro, Baneriji wrote to Marshall about the site, postulating an origin in "remote antiquity," and noting a congruence of some of its artifacts with those of Harappa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20099_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20099-70">[50]</a></sup> Later in 1923, Vats, also in correspondence with Marshall, noted the same more specifically about the seals and the script found at both sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20099_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20099-70">[50]</a></sup> On the weight of these opinions, Marshall ordered crucial data from the two sites to be brought to one location and invited Banerji and Sahni to a joint discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20099–10_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20099–10-71">[51]</a></sup> By 1924, Marshall had become convinced of the significance of the finds, and on 24 September 1924, made a tentative but conspicuous public intimation in the <i>Illustrated London News</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright200910_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright200910-29">[21]</a></sup> </p><blockquote><p> "Not often has it been given to archaeologists, as it was given to <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Schliemann</a> at <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a>, or to <a href="/wiki/Aurel_Stein" title="Aurel Stein">Stein</a> in the deserts of <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>, to light upon the remains of a long forgotten civilisation. It looks, however, at this moment, as if we were on the threshold of such a discovery in the plains of the Indus."</p></blockquote>
<p>In the next issue, a week later, the British Assyriologist <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Sayce" title="Archibald Sayce">Archibald Sayce</a> was able to point to very similar seals found in Bronze Age levels in Mesopotamia and Iran, giving the first strong indication of their date; confirmations from other archaeologists followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPossehl20023_and_12_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPossehl20023_and_12-72">[52]</a></sup> Systematic excavations began in Mohenjo-daro in 1924–25 with that of <a href="/wiki/Kashinath_Narayan_Dikshit" title="Kashinath Narayan Dikshit">K. N. Dikshit</a>, continuing with those of H. Hargreaves (1925–1926), and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_J._H._Mackay" title="Ernest J. H. Mackay">Ernest J. H. Mackay</a> (1927–1931).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20098–9_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20098–9-69">[49]</a></sup> By 1931, much of Mohenjo-daro had been excavated, but occasional excavations continued, such as the one led by <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Wheeler" title="Mortimer Wheeler">Mortimer Wheeler</a>, a new director-general of the ASI appointed in 1944, and including <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Hasan_Dani" title="Ahmad Hasan Dani">Ahmad Hasan Dani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joffe_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-joffe-73">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the partition of India in 1947, when most excavated sites of the Indus Valley Civilisation lay in territory awarded to Pakistan, the Archaeological Survey of India, its area of authority reduced, carried out large numbers of surveys and excavations along the Ghaggar-Hakra system in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guha_mas_2005_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-guha_mas_2005-74">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[u]</a></sup> Some speculated that the Ghaggar-Hakra system might yield more sites than the Indus river basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert2017_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert2017-76">[55]</a></sup> According to archaeologist Ratnagar, many Ghaggar-Hakra sites in India and Indus Valley sites in Pakistan are actually those of local cultures; some sites display contact with Harappan civilisation, but only a few are fully developed Harappan ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERatnagar2006b7–8_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERatnagar2006b7–8-77">[56]</a></sup> As of 1977,  about 90% of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">Indus script seals and inscribed objects</a> discovered were found at sites in Pakistan along the Indus river, while other sites accounts only for the remaining 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[58]</a></sup> By 2002, over 1,000 Mature Harappan cities and settlements had been reported, of which just under a hundred had been excavated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorrisonJunker2002_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-MorrisonJunker2002-18">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPossehl200220_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPossehl200220-20">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008-p137_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-p137-21">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConinghamYoung2015_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ConinghamYoung2015-81">[59]</a></sup> mainly in the general region of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus" title="Indus">Indus</a> and Ghaggar-Hakra rivers and their tributaries; however, there are only five major urban sites: <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dholavira" title="Dholavira">Dholavira</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganeriwala" title="Ganeriwala">Ganeriwala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rakhigarhi" title="Rakhigarhi">Rakhigarhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConinghamYoung2015_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-ConinghamYoung2015-81">[59]</a></sup> As of 2008, about 616 sites have been reported in India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-34">[24]</a></sup> whereas 406 sites have been reported in Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-34">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike India, in which after 1947, the ASI attempted to "Indianise" archaeological work in keeping with the new nation's goals of national unity and historical continuity, in Pakistan the national imperative was the promotion of Islamic heritage, and consequently archaeological work on early sites was left to foreign archaeologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichon2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid675cCgAAQBAJpgPT44_44ff]_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichon2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid675cCgAAQBAJpgPT44_44ff]-82">[60]</a></sup> After the partition, Mortimer Wheeler, the Director of ASI from 1944, oversaw the establishment of archaeological institutions in Pakistan, later joining a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> effort tasked to conserve the site at Mohenjo-daro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201585_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201585-83">[61]</a></sup> Other international efforts at Mohenjo-daro and Harappa have included the German <i><a href="/wiki/RWTH_Aachen_University" title="RWTH Aachen University">Aachen</a> Research Project Mohenjo-daro</i>, the <i>Italian Mission to Mohenjo-daro</i>, and the US <i>Harappa Archaeological Research Project (HARP)</i> founded by <a href="/wiki/George_F._Dales" title="George F. Dales">George F. Dales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright200914_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright200914-84">[62]</a></sup> Following a chance flash flood which exposed a portion of an archaeological site at the foot of the <a href="/wiki/Bolan_Pass" title="Bolan Pass">Bolan Pass</a> in <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a>, excavations were carried out in <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> by French archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Jarrige" title="Jean-François Jarrige">Jean-François Jarrige</a> and his team in the early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015109_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015109-85">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chronology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodisation_of_the_Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Periodisation of the Indus Valley civilisation">Periodisation of the Indus Valley civilisation</a></div>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-right:4em;"><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_South_Asian_history" title="Outline of South Asian history">Outline</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks plainlist"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;padding-right:4em;"><a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Stone_Age" title="South Asian Stone Age">Palaeolithic</a> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047488">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold">(2,500,000–250,000 BC)</span></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><table style="width:100%;border-collapse:collapse;border-spacing:0px 0px;border:none"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;"> <a href="/wiki/Madrasian_culture" title="Madrasian culture">Madrasian culture</a></td><td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;"> <a href="/wiki/Soanian" title="Soanian">Soanian culture</a></td><td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_South_Asia" title="Template:History of South Asia"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_South_Asia" title="Template talk:History of South Asia"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_South_Asia&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The cities of the ancient Indus had "social hierarchies, their writing system, their large planned cities and their long-distance trade [which] mark them to archaeologists as a full-fledged 'civilisation.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandler_34–42_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandler_34–42-86">[64]</a></sup> The mature phase of the Harappan civilisation lasted from c. 2600–1900 BCE. With the inclusion of the predecessor and successor cultures – Early Harappan and Late Harappan, respectively – the entire Indus Valley Civilisation may be taken to have lasted from the 33rd to the 14th centuries BCE. It is part of the Indus Valley Tradition, which also includes the pre-Harappan occupation of Mehrgarh, the earliest farming site of the Indus Valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenoyer1991_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenoyer1991-16">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201527_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201527-87">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several periodisations are employed for the IVC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenoyer1991_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenoyer1991-16">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201527_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201527-87">[65]</a></sup> The most commonly used classifies the Indus Valley Civilisation into Early, Mature and Late Harappan Phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201525_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung201525-88">[66]</a></sup> An alternative approach by Shaffer divides the broader Indus Valley Tradition into four eras, the pre-Harappan "Early Food Producing Era", and the Regionalisation, Integration, and Localisation eras, which correspond roughly with the Early Harappan, Mature Harappan, and Late Harappan phases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaffer_1992_loc=I:441–464,_II:425–446_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shaffer_1992_loc=I:441–464,_II:425–446-15">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManuel2010148_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManuel2010148-89">[67]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Harappan_era:_Mehrgarh">Pre-Harappan era: Mehrgarh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pre-Harappan era: Mehrgarh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> (7000 BCE to c. 2500 BCE) mountain site in the Balochistan province of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[76]</a></sup> which gave new insights on the emergence of the Indus Valley Civilisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandler_34–42_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chandler_34–42-86">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[w]</a></sup> Mehrgarh is one of the earliest sites with evidence of farming and herding in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[78]</a></sup> Mehrgarh was influenced by the Near Eastern Neolithic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGangalSarsonShukurov2014_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGangalSarsonShukurov2014-102">[79]</a></sup> with similarities between "domesticated wheat varieties, early phases of farming, pottery, other archaeological artefacts, some domesticated plants and herd animals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESingh,_Sakshi2016_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh,_Sakshi2016-103">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Near_East_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Near_East-104">[x]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Jarrige" title="Jean-François Jarrige">Jean-Francois Jarrige</a> argues for an independent origin of Mehrgarh. Jarrige notes "the assumption that farming economy was introduced full-fledged from Near-East to South Asia,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJarrige2008a_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJarrige2008a-105">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Near_East_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Near_East-104">[x]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[y]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[z]</a></sup> and the similarities between Neolithic sites from eastern Mesopotamia and the western Indus valley, which are evidence of a "cultural continuum" between those sites. But given the originality of Mehrgarh, Jarrige concludes that Mehrgarh has an earlier local background, and is not a "'backwater' of the Neolithic culture of the Near East".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJarrige2008a_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJarrige2008a-105">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lukacs and Hemphill suggest an initial local development of Mehrgarh, with a continuity in cultural development but a change in population. According to Lukacs and Hemphill, while there is a strong continuity between the neolithic and <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">chalcolithic</a> (Copper Age) cultures of Mehrgarh, dental evidence shows that the chalcolithic population did not descend from the neolithic population of Mehrgarh,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015114_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015114-121">[95]</a></sup> which "suggests moderate levels of gene flow."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015114_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConinghamYoung2015114-121">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[aa]</a></sup> Mascarenhas et al. (2015) note that "new, possibly West Asian, body types are reported from the graves of Mehrgarh beginning in the Togau phase (3800 BCE)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMascarenhasRainaAstonSanghera20159_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMascarenhasRainaAstonSanghera20159-123">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gallego Romero et al. (2011) state that their research on lactose tolerance in India suggests that "the west Eurasian genetic contribution identified by Reich et al. (2009) principally reflects gene flow from Iran and the Middle East."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGallego_Romero20119_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGallego_Romero20119-124">[97]</a></sup> They further note that "[t]he earliest evidence of cattle herding in south Asia comes from the Indus River Valley site of Mehrgarh and is dated to 7,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YBP" title="YBP">YBP</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGallego_Romero20119_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGallego_Romero20119-124">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[ab]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Harappan">Early Harappan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early Harappan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_Civilization,_Early_Phase_(3300-2600_BCE).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="733" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Early_Phase_%283300-2600_BCE%29.png/220px-Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Early_Phase_%283300-2600_BCE%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Early_Phase_%283300-2600_BCE%29.png/330px-Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Early_Phase_%283300-2600_BCE%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Early_Phase_%283300-2600_BCE%29.png/440px-Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Early_Phase_%283300-2600_BCE%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_Civilization,_Early_Phase_(3300-2600_BCE).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early Harappan Period, c. 3300–2600 BCE</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valle_dell%27indo,_barca_a_forma_di_toro,_periodo_kot-dijan,_2800-2600_ac_ca._(coll._priv.)_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="2552" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Valle_dell%27indo%2C_barca_a_forma_di_toro%2C_periodo_kot-dijan%2C_2800-2600_ac_ca._%28coll._priv.%29_02.jpg/220px-Valle_dell%27indo%2C_barca_a_forma_di_toro%2C_periodo_kot-dijan%2C_2800-2600_ac_ca._%28coll._priv.%29_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Valle_dell%27indo%2C_barca_a_forma_di_toro%2C_periodo_kot-dijan%2C_2800-2600_ac_ca._%28coll._priv.%29_02.jpg/330px-Valle_dell%27indo%2C_barca_a_forma_di_toro%2C_periodo_kot-dijan%2C_2800-2600_ac_ca._%28coll._priv.%29_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Valle_dell%27indo%2C_barca_a_forma_di_toro%2C_periodo_kot-dijan%2C_2800-2600_ac_ca._%28coll._priv.%29_02.jpg/440px-Valle_dell%27indo%2C_barca_a_forma_di_toro%2C_periodo_kot-dijan%2C_2800-2600_ac_ca._%28coll._priv.%29_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Valle_dell%27indo,_barca_a_forma_di_toro,_periodo_kot-dijan,_2800-2600_ac_ca._(coll._priv.)_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Terracotta boat in the shape of a bull, and female figurines. <a href="/wiki/Kot_Diji" title="Kot Diji">Kot Diji</a> period (c. 2800–2600 BC).</div></div></div>
<p>The Early Harappan Ravi Phase, named after the nearby <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi River</a>, lasted from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 3300</span> BCE until 2800 BCE. It started when farmers from the mountains gradually moved between their mountain homes and the lowland river valleys,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[99]</a></sup> and is related to the <a href="/wiki/Hakra_Ware_culture" title="Hakra Ware culture">Hakra Phase</a>, identified in the Ghaggar-Hakra River Valley to the west, and predates the <a href="/wiki/Kot_Diji" title="Kot Diji">Kot Diji</a> Phase (2800–2600 BCE, Harappan 2), named after a site in northern <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, Pakistan, near <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a>. The earliest examples of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">Indus script</a> date to the 3rd millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mature phase of earlier village cultures is represented by <a href="/wiki/Rehman_Dheri" title="Rehman Dheri">Rehman Dheri</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amri_(Pre-Harappa)" title="Amri (Pre-Harappa)">Amri</a> in Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kot_Diji" title="Kot Diji">Kot Diji</a> represents the phase leading up to Mature Harappan, with the citadel representing centralised authority and an increasingly urban quality of life. Another town of this stage was found at <a href="/wiki/Kalibangan" title="Kalibangan">Kalibangan</a> in India on the Hakra River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade networks linked this culture with related regional cultures and distant sources of raw materials, including <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> and other materials for bead-making. By this time, villagers had domesticated numerous crops, including <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesame_seed" title="Sesame seed">sesame seeds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Date_(fruit)" title="Date (fruit)">dates</a>, and cotton, as well as animals, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_buffalo" title="Domestic buffalo">water buffalo</a>. Early Harappan communities turned to large urban centres by 2600 BCE, from where the mature Harappan phase started. The latest research shows that Indus Valley people migrated from villages to cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final stages of the Early Harappan period are characterised by the building of large walled settlements, the expansion of trade networks, and the increasing integration of regional communities into a "relatively uniform" material culture in terms of pottery styles, ornaments, and <a href="/wiki/Stamp_seal" title="Stamp seal">stamp seals</a> with <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">Indus script</a>, leading into the transition to the Mature Harappan phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenoyer2006_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenoyer2006-134">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mature_Harappan">Mature Harappan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_Valley_Civilisation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mature Harappan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_Civilization,_Mature_Phase_(2600-1900_BCE).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="733" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Mature_Phase_%282600-1900_BCE%29.png/330px-Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Mature_Phase_%282600-1900_BCE%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Mature_Phase_%282600-1900_BCE%29.png/495px-Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Mature_Phase_%282600-1900_BCE%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Mature_Phase_%282600-1900_BCE%29.png/660px-Indus_Valley_Civilization%2C_Mature_Phase_%282600-1900_BCE%29.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_Civilization,_Mature_Phase_(2600-1900_BCE).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mature Harappan Period, c. 2600–1900 BCE</div></div></div>

.trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle
